			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Holocene extinction</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">

<div class="thumbcaption">The percentage of marine animal extinction at the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> level through the five <a href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" class="mw-redirect" title="Mass extinction">mass extinctions</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>Holocene extinction</b>, otherwise referred to as the <b>Sixth extinction</b> or <b><a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">Anthropocene</a> extinction</b>, is the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">extinction event</a> of <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> during the present <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(geology)" title="Epoch (geology)">epoch</a> mainly due to human activity. The large number of extinctions span numerous families of <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a> including <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>. with widespread degradation of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_hotspot" title="Biodiversity hotspot">highly biodiverse</a> habitats such as <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforest</a>, as well as other areas, the vast majority are thought to be undocumented. According to the <a href="/wiki/Species-area_curve" class="mw-redirect" title="Species-area curve">species-area theory</a> and based on upper-bound estimating, the present rate of extinction may be up to 140,000 species per year, making it the greatest loss of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> since the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a>.</p>
<p>The Holocene extinction includes the disappearance of large land animals known as <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a>, starting at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last Ice Age</a>. Megafauna outside of the <a href="/wiki/African_continent" class="mw-redirect" title="African continent">African continent</a>, which did not evolve alongside humans, proved highly sensitive to the introduction of new predation, and many died out shortly after early humans began spreading and hunting across the Earth (additionally, many <a href="/wiki/List_of_African_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of African animals extinct in the Holocene">African species have also gone extinct in the Holocene</a>). These extinctions, occurring near the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>–<a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> boundary, are sometimes referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_extinction_event" title="Quaternary extinction event">Quaternary extinction event</a>.</p>
<p>The arrival of humans on different continents coincide with megafaunal extinction. The most popular theory is that human overhunting species added to existing stress conditions. Although there is debate on how much human predation affected their decline, certain population declines have been directly correlated with human activity, such as the extinction events of <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_New_Zealand" title="List of extinct animals of New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_the_Hawaiian_Islands" title="List of extinct animals of the Hawaiian Islands">Hawaii</a>. Aside from humans, climate change may have been a driving factor in the megafaunal extinctions, especially at the end of the Quaternary.</p>
<p>The ecology of humanity has been noted as being that of an unprecedented "global superpredator" that regularly preys on the adults of other <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predators</a> and has <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">worldwide effects</a> on <a href="/wiki/Food_web" title="Food web">food webs</a>. Extinctions of species have occurred on every <a href="/wiki/Land_mass" class="mw-redirect" title="Land mass">land mass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">ocean</a>, with many famous examples <a href="/wiki/List_of_African_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of African animals extinct in the Holocene">within Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Asian_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of Asian animals extinct in the Holocene">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_Europe" title="List of extinct animals of Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_Australia" title="List of extinct animals of Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_North_American_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of North American animals extinct in the Holocene">North</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_South_American_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of South American animals extinct in the Holocene">South America</a>, and on smaller islands. Overall, the Holocene extinction can be characterized by the <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">human impact on the environment</a>. The Holocene extinction continues into the 21st century, with <a href="/wiki/Meat#Environmental_impact" title="Meat">meat consumption</a>, <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Decline_in_amphibian_populations" title="Decline in amphibian populations">amphibian crisis</a> being a few broader examples of an almost universal, <a href="/wiki/Cosmopolitan_distribution" title="Cosmopolitan distribution">cosmopolitan</a> <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment#Biodiversity" title="Human impact on the environment">decline in biodiversity</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">ballooning human population</a> along with profligate <a href="/wiki/Consumption_(economics)" title="Consumption (economics)">consumption</a> are considered to be the primary drivers of this rapid decline.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Holocene extinction is also known as the "Sixth extinction", due to it being the sixth mass extinct event, after the <a href="/wiki/Ordovician%E2%80%93Silurian_extinction_events" title="Ordovician–Silurian extinction events">Ordovician–Silurian extinction events</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Late_Devonian_extinction" title="Late Devonian extinction">Late Devonian extinction</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Triassic%E2%80%93Jurassic_extinction_event" title="Triassic–Jurassic extinction event">Triassic–Jurassic extinction event</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ceballos_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ceballos-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dirzo_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dirzo-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zalasiewicz2015_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zalasiewicz2015-4">[4]</a></sup> There is no general agreement on where the Holocene, or <a href="/wiki/Anthropogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthropogenic">anthropogenic</a>, extinction begins, and the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_extinction_event" title="Quaternary extinction event">Quaternary extinction event</a>, which includes climate change resulting in the end of the last <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice age</a>, ends, or if they should be considered separate events at all.<sup id="cite_ref-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-link.springer.com_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-link.springer.com-6">[6]</a></sup> Some have suggested that anthropogenic extinctions may have begun as early as when the first modern humans spread out of Africa between 100,000 and 200,000 years ago, which is supported by rapid megafaunal extinction following recent human colonisation in <a href="/wiki/Australian_megafauna" title="Australian megafauna">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_New_Zealand" title="List of extinct animals of New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_African_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of African animals extinct in the Holocene">Madagascar</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup> in a similar way that any <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">large, adaptable predator moving into a new ecosystem would</a>. In many cases, it is suggested even minimal hunting pressure was enough to wipe out large fauna, particularly on <a href="/wiki/Geographical_isolation" class="mw-redirect" title="Geographical isolation">geographically isolated</a> islands.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-8">[8]</a></sup> Only during the most recent parts of the extinction have <a href="/wiki/Effect_of_climate_change_on_plant_biodiversity" class="mw-redirect" title="Effect of climate change on plant biodiversity">plants also suffered large losses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <i>The Future of Life</i> (2002), <a href="/wiki/E.O._Wilson" class="mw-redirect" title="E.O. Wilson">E.O. Wilson</a> of Harvard calculated that, if the current rate of human disruption of the biosphere continues, one-half of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s higher lifeforms will be extinct by 2100. A 1998 poll conducted by the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a> found that seventy percent of biologists acknowledge the existence of the anthropogenic extinction.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Numerous scientific studies — such as a 2004 report published in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-MSNBC_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC-11">[11]</a></sup> and papers authored by the <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a>'s annual <a href="/wiki/Red_List" class="mw-redirect" title="Red List">Red List</a> of threatened species — have since reinforced this conviction. At present, the rate of extinction of species is estimated at 100 to 1,000 times higher than the "<a href="/wiki/Background_extinction_rate" title="Background extinction rate">base</a>" or historically typical rate of extinction (in terms of the natural evolution of the planet)<sup id="cite_ref-:1_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lawton95_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lawton95-13">[13]</a></sup> and also the current rate of extinction is, therefore, 10 to 100 times higher than any of the previous <a href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" class="mw-redirect" title="Mass extinction">mass extinctions</a> in the history of Earth. It is also the only known mass extinction of plants.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> One scientist estimates the current extinction rate may be 10,000 times the <a href="/wiki/Background_extinction_rate" title="Background extinction rate">background extinction rate</a>. Nevertheless, most scientists predict a much lower extinction rate than this outlying estimate.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Pimm" title="Stuart Pimm">Stuart Pimm</a> stated "the current rate of species extinction is about 100 times the natural rate" for plants.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Mass extinctions are characterized by the loss of at least 75% of species within a geologically short period of time.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a pair of studies published in 2015, extrapolation from observed extinction of Hawaiian snails led to the conclusion that 7% of all species on Earth may have been lost already.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropocene">Anthropocene</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Anthropocene">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">Anthropocene</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ecological_Processes_Coral_Reefs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Ecological_Processes_Coral_Reefs.jpg/220px-Ecological_Processes_Coral_Reefs.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Ecological_Processes_Coral_Reefs.jpg/330px-Ecological_Processes_Coral_Reefs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Ecological_Processes_Coral_Reefs.jpg/440px-Ecological_Processes_Coral_Reefs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3508" data-file-height="2481" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ecological_Processes_Coral_Reefs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A diagram showing the ecological processes of <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a> before and after the <a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">Anthropocene</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The abundance of species extinctions considered <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">anthropogenic</a>, or due to human activity, have sometimes (especially when referring to hypothesized future events) been collectively called the "Anthropocene extinction".<sup id="cite_ref-wooldridge2008_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wooldridge2008-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jackson2008_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jackson2008-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SpecialIssue_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SpecialIssue-22">[22]</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">Anthropocene</a>" is a term introduced in 2000. It is now posited by some that a new geological epoch has begun, characterised by the most abrupt and widespread extinction of species since the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a> 66 million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The term "<a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">anthropocene</a>" is used by few scientists, and some commentators may refer to the current and projected future extinctions as part of a longer Holocene extinction.<sup id="cite_ref-Zalasiewicz_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zalasiewicz-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The Holocene–Anthropocene boundary is contested, with some commentators asserting significant human influence on climate for much of what is normally regarded as the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(geology)" title="Epoch (geology)">Epoch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:102_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:102-25">[25]</a></sup> Other commentators place the Holocene–Anthropocene boundary at the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a> while also saying that, "[f]ormal adoption of this term in the near future will largely depend on its utility, particularly to earth scientists working on late Holocene successions."</p>
<p>It has been suggested that human activity has made the period following the mid-20th century different enough from the rest of the Holocene to consider it a new <a href="/wiki/Geological_epoch" class="mw-redirect" title="Geological epoch">geological epoch</a>, known as the <a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">Anthropocene</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> which was considered for implementation into the timeline of Earth's history by the <a href="/wiki/International_Commission_on_Stratigraphy" title="International Commission on Stratigraphy">International Commission on Stratigraphy</a> in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In order to constitute the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">extinction event</a>, scientists must determine exactly when anthropogenic <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas emissions</a> began to measurably alter natural atmospheric levels at a global scale and when these alterations caused changes to global climate. Employing chemical proxies from Antarctic ice cores, researchers have estimated the fluctuations of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane gases</a> (CH<sub>4</sub>) in the earth’s atmosphere for the <a href="/wiki/Late_Pleistocene" title="Late Pleistocene">late Pleistocene</a> and Holocene epochs.<sup id="cite_ref-:102_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:102-25">[25]</a></sup> Based on studies that estimated fluctuations of carbon dioxide and methane in the atmosphere using chemical proxies from Antarctic ice cores, general argumentation of when the peak of the Anthropocene occurred pertains to the timeframe within the previous two centuries; typically beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, when greenhouse gas levels were recorded by contemporary methods at its highest.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Competition_by_humans">Competition by humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Competition by humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Megafauna#Megafaunal_mass_extinctions" title="Megafauna">Megafaunal mass extinctions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_extinction_event" title="Quaternary extinction event">Quaternary extinction event</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:443px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Extinctions_Africa_Austrailia_NAmerica_Madagascar.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ad/Extinctions_Africa_Austrailia_NAmerica_Madagascar.gif" width="441" height="452" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="441" data-file-height="452" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">The percent of <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a> on different land masses over time, with the arrival of humans indicated.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Holocene extinction is mainly caused by human activity.<sup id="cite_ref-ceballos_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ceballos-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dirzo_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dirzo-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SpecialIssue_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SpecialIssue-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zalasiewicz2015_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zalasiewicz2015-4">[4]</a></sup> Extinction of animals, plants, and other organisms caused by human actions may go as far back as the late <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>, over 12,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-VanishingFauna_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VanishingFauna-31">[31]</a></sup> There is a correlation between megafaunal extinction and the arrival of humans, and <a href="/wiki/Human_population_growth" class="mw-redirect" title="Human population growth">human population growth</a>, most prominently in the past two centuries, is regarded as one of the underlying causes of extinction.<sup id="cite_ref-ceballos_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ceballos-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PimmJenkins_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PimmJenkins-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sutter_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sutter-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">Megafauna</a> was once found on every continent of the world and large islands such as <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, but is now almost exclusively found on the continent of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, with notable comparisons on <a href="/wiki/Australian_megafauna" title="Australian megafauna">Australia</a> and the islands previously mentioned experiences population crashes and <a href="/wiki/Trophic_cascade" title="Trophic cascade">trophic cascades</a> shortly after the earliest human settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-8">[8]</a></sup> It has been suggested that the African megafauna survived as they evolved alongside humans.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup> The timing of <a href="/wiki/List_of_South_American_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of South American animals extinct in the Holocene">South American megafaunal extinction</a> does not appear to correspond to human arrival, although the possibility of whether human activity at the time may have impacted the global climate enough to cause such an extinction has been suggested.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup> It has been noted, in the face of such evidence, humans are unique in ecology as an unprecedented 'global superpredator', regularly preying on large numbers of fully grown terrestrial and marine <a href="/wiki/Apex_predators" class="mw-redirect" title="Apex predators">apex predators</a>, and with a great deal of influence over food webs and climatic systems worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-35">[35]</a></sup> Although significant debate exists as to how much human predation and indirect effects contributed to prehistoric extinctions, certain population crashes have been directly correlated with human arrival.<sup id="cite_ref-link.springer.com_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-link.springer.com-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-VanishingFauna_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VanishingFauna-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Human civilization flourished in accordance to the efficiency and intensification of prevailing <a href="/wiki/Subsistence_agriculture" title="Subsistence agriculture">subsistence systems</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-36">[36]</a></sup> Local communities that acquire more subsistence strategies increased in number to combat competitive pressures of land utilization.<sup id="cite_ref-:102_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:102-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:15_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-36">[36]</a></sup> Therefore, the Holocene developed competition on the basis of agriculture. The growth of agriculture has then introduced newer means of climate change, pollution, and ecological development.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg/220px-Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg/330px-Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg/440px-Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1605" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Dodo" title="Dodo">dodo</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Flightless_bird" title="Flightless bird">flightless bird</a> native to <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a>, became <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinct</a> during the mid- to late <a href="/wiki/17th_century" title="17th century">17th century</a> due to habitat destruction and predation by introduced mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction#Human_causes" title="Habitat destruction">Habitat destruction by humans</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Marine_resources_conservation#Human_impact" title="Marine resources conservation">oceanic devastation</a>, such as through <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a> and contamination; and the modification and destruction of vast tracts of land and river systems around the world to meet solely human-centered ends (with 13 percent of Earth's ice-free land surface now used as row-crop agricultural sites, 26 percent used as pastures, and 4 percent urban-industrial areas<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>), thus replacing the original local ecosystems.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Other, related human causes of the extinction event include <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> the introduction in various regions of <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">non-native species</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Globalization_and_disease" title="Globalization and disease">widespread</a> <a href="/wiki/Transmission_(medicine)" title="Transmission (medicine)">transmission</a> of <a href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Infectious disease">infectious diseases</a> spread through livestock and crops.<sup id="cite_ref-lawton95_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lawton95-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recent investigations about <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> landscape burning has a major implication for the current debate about the timing of the Anthropocene and the role that humans may have played in the production of greenhouse gases prior to the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-36">[36]</a></sup> Studies on early <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a> raises questions about the current use of population size or density as a <a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">proxy</a> for the amount of land clearance and anthropogenic burning that took place in pre-industrial times.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:16_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16-43">[43]</a></sup> Scientists have questioned the correlation between population size and early territorial alterations.<sup id="cite_ref-:16_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16-43">[43]</a></sup> Ruddiman and Ellis' research paper in 2009 makes the case that early farmers involved in systems of agriculture used more land per capita than growers later in the Holocene, who intensified their labor to produce more food per unit of area (thus, per laborer); arguing that agricultural involvement in rice production implemented thousands of years ago by relatively small populations have created significant environmental impacts through large-scale means of deforestation.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>While a number of human-derived factors are recognized as potentially contributing to rising atmospheric concentrations of CH<sub>4</sub> and CO<sub>2</sub>, deforestation and territorial clearance practices associated with agricultural development may be contributing most to these concentrations globally.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:13_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:15_36-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-36">[36]</a></sup> Scientists that are employing a variance of <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeological</a> and paleoecological data argue that the processes contributing to substantial human modification of the environment spanned many thousands of years ago on a global scale and thus, not originating as early as the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. Gaining popularity on his uncommon hypothesis, Palaeoclimatologist <a href="/wiki/William_Ruddiman" title="William Ruddiman">William Ruddiman</a> in 2003, stipulated that in the early Holocene 11,000 years ago, atmospheric carbon dioxide and methane levels has fluctuated in a pattern which was different from the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> epoch before it.<sup id="cite_ref-:102_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:102-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:12_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:13_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-44">[44]</a></sup> He argued that the patterns of the significant decline of CO<sub>2</sub> levels during the last ice age of the Pleistocene inversely correlates to the Holocene where there has been dramatic increases of CO<sub>2</sub> around 8000 years ago and CH<sub>4</sub> levels 3000 years after that.<sup id="cite_ref-:13_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-44">[44]</a></sup> The correlation between the decrease of CO<sub>2</sub> in the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> and the increase of it during the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> implies that the causation of this spark of greenhouse gases into the atmosphere was the growth of <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">human agriculture</a> during the Holocene such as the anthropogenic expansion of (human) land use and <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:102_25-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:102-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:13_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Islands">Islands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Islands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Human arrival in the Caribbean around 6,000 years ago is correlated with the extinction of many species.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Examples include many different genera of <a href="/wiki/Pilosans_of_the_Caribbean" title="Pilosans of the Caribbean">ground and arboreal sloths</a> across all islands. These sloths were generally smaller than those found on the South American continent. <i><a href="/wiki/Megalocnus" title="Megalocnus">Megalocnus</a></i> were the largest genus at up to 90 kilograms (200&#160;lb), <i><a href="/wiki/Acratocnus" title="Acratocnus">Acratocnus</a></i> were medium-sized relatives of modern <a href="/wiki/Two-toed_sloth" title="Two-toed sloth">two-toed sloths</a> endemic to <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Imagocnus" title="Imagocnus">Imagocnus</a></i> also of Cuba, <i><a href="/wiki/Neocnus" title="Neocnus">Neocnus</a></i> and many others.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recent research, based on <a href="/wiki/Archeology" class="mw-redirect" title="Archeology">archaeological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontological</a> digs on 70 different Pacific islands has shown that numerous species became extinct as people moved across the Pacific, starting 30,000 years ago in the <a href="/wiki/Bismarck_Archipelago" title="Bismarck Archipelago">Bismarck Archipelago</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">Solomon Islands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> It is currently estimated that among the bird species of the Pacific, some 2000 species have gone extinct since the arrival of humans, representing a 20% drop in the biodiversity of birds worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Hawaii" title="Ancient Hawaii">first settlers</a> are thought to have arrived in the islands between 300 and 800 CE, with European arrival in the 16th century. Hawaii is notable for its <a href="/wiki/Endemism_in_the_Hawaiian_Islands" title="Endemism in the Hawaiian Islands">endemism</a> of plants, <a href="/wiki/Endemic_birds_of_Hawaii" title="Endemic birds of Hawaii">birds</a>, insects, mollusks and <a href="/wiki/List_of_fish_of_Hawaii" title="List of fish of Hawaii">fish</a>; 30% of its organisms are endemic. Many of its species are endangered or have gone extinct, primarily due to accidentally introduced species and livestock grazing. Over 40% of its bird species have gone extinct, and it is the location of 75% of extinctions in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Extinction has increased in Hawaii over the last 200 years and is relatively well documented, with extinctions among native snails used as estimates for global extinction rates.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Australian_megafauna" title="Australian megafauna">Australian megafauna</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_Australia" title="List of extinct animals of Australia">List of extinct animals of Australia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_flora_of_Australia" title="List of extinct flora of Australia">List of extinct flora of Australia</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species_in_Australia" title="Invasive species in Australia">Invasive species in Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Land_clearing_in_Australia" title="Land clearing in Australia">Land clearing in Australia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fire-stick_farming" title="Fire-stick farming">Fire-stick farming</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Genyornis_BW.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Genyornis_BW.jpg/220px-Genyornis_BW.jpg" width="220" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Genyornis_BW.jpg/330px-Genyornis_BW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Genyornis_BW.jpg/440px-Genyornis_BW.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="1002" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Genyornis_BW.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Genyornis_newtoni" class="mw-redirect" title="Genyornis newtoni">Genyornis newtoni</a></i>, a 7 foot tall flightless bird. Evidence of egg cooking in this species is the first evidence of megafaunal hunting by humans on Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-50">[50]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> was once home to a <a href="/wiki/Australian_megafauna" title="Australian megafauna">large assemblage of megafauna</a>, with many parallels to those found on the African continent today. Australia's fauna is characterised by primarily <a href="/wiki/Marsupial" title="Marsupial">marsupial</a> mammals, and many reptiles and birds, all existing as giant forms until recently. <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Humans</a> arrived on the continent very early, about 50,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup> The extent human arrival contributed is controversial; climatic drying of Australia 40,000–60,000 years ago was an unlikely cause, as it was less severe in speed or magnitude than previous regional climate change which failed to kill off megafauna. Extinctions in Australia continued from original settlement until today in both <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_flora_of_Australia" title="List of extinct flora of Australia">plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_Australia" title="List of extinct animals of Australia">animals</a>, whilst <a href="/wiki/Threatened_fauna_of_Australia" title="Threatened fauna of Australia">many more animals</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_threatened_flora_of_Australia" title="List of threatened flora of Australia">plants</a> have declined or are endangered.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Due to the older timeframe and the soil chemistry on the continent, very little <a href="/wiki/Subfossil" title="Subfossil">subfossil</a> preservation evidence exists relative to elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-51">[51]</a></sup> However, continent-wide extinction of all genera weighing over 100 kilograms, and six of seven genera weighing between 45 and 100 kilograms occurred around 46,400 years ago (4,000 years after human arrival)<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and the fact that megafauna survived until a later date on the island of <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a> following the establishment of a land bridge<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> suggest direct hunting or anthropogenic ecosystem disruption such as <a href="/wiki/Fire-stick_farming" title="Fire-stick farming">fire-stick farming</a> as likely causes. The first evidence of direct human predation leading to extinction in Australia was published in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Madagascar">Madagascar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Madagascar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Madagascar" title="Wildlife of Madagascar">Wildlife of Madagascar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subfossil_lemur" title="Subfossil lemur">Subfossil lemur</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:413px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Subfossil_lemur_C14_ranges.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Subfossil_lemur_C14_ranges.svg/411px-Subfossil_lemur_C14_ranges.svg.png" width="411" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Subfossil_lemur_C14_ranges.svg/617px-Subfossil_lemur_C14_ranges.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Subfossil_lemur_C14_ranges.svg/822px-Subfossil_lemur_C14_ranges.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="875" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Subfossil_lemur_C14_ranges.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Radiocarbon dating of multiple subfossil specimens shows that now extinct <a href="/wiki/Subfossil_lemur" title="Subfossil lemur">giant lemurs</a> were present in Madagascar until after human arrival.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Within 500 years of the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Malagasy_people" title="Malagasy people">humans</a> between 2,500–2,000 years ago, nearly all of Madagascar's distinct, <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allopatric_speciation" title="Allopatric speciation">geographically isolated</a> megafauna became extinct.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The largest animals, of more than 150 kilograms (330&#160;lb), were extinct very shortly after the first human arrival, with large and medium-sized species dying out after prolonged hunting pressure from an expanding human population moving into more remote regions of the island around 1000 years ago. Smaller fauna experienced initial increases due to decreased competition, and then subsequent declines over the last 500 years.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-8">[8]</a></sup> All fauna weighing over 10 kilograms (22&#160;lb) died out. The primary reasons for this are human hunting and <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Madagascar" title="Deforestation in Madagascar">habitat loss</a> from early <a href="/wiki/Aridification" title="Aridification">aridification</a>, both of which persist and threaten Madagascar's remaining taxa today.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The eight or more species of <a href="/wiki/Elephant_bird" title="Elephant bird">elephant birds</a>, giant flightless <a href="/wiki/Ratite" title="Ratite">ratites</a> in the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Aepyornis" title="Aepyornis">Aepyornis</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Mullerornis" title="Mullerornis">Mullerornis</a></i>, are extinct from over-hunting,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> as well as 17 species of lemur, known as giant, <a href="/wiki/Subfossil_lemur" title="Subfossil lemur">subfossil lemurs</a>. Some of these lemurs typically weighed over 150 kilograms (330&#160;lb), and fossils have provided evidence of human butchery on many species.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Zealand">New Zealand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: New Zealand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_New_Zealand" title="List of extinct animals of New Zealand">List of extinct animals of New Zealand</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_of_New_Zealand" title="Biodiversity of New Zealand">Biodiversity of New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_New_Zealand_environment" title="Timeline of the New Zealand environment">Timeline of the New Zealand environment</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species_in_New_Zealand" title="Invasive species in New Zealand">Invasive species in New Zealand</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> is characterised by its <a href="/wiki/Geographic_isolation" class="mw-redirect" title="Geographic isolation">geographic isolation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insular_biogeography" title="Insular biogeography">island biogeography</a>, and had been isolated from mainland Australia for 80 million years. It was the last large land mass to be colonised by humans. The arrival of <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesian</a> settlers circa 12th century resulted in the extinction of all of the islands' megafaunal birds within several hundred years.<sup id="cite_ref-kolbert_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kolbert-57">[57]</a></sup> The last <a href="/wiki/Moa" title="Moa">moa</a>, large flightless <a href="/wiki/Ratite" title="Ratite">ratites</a>, became extinct within 200 years of the arrival of human settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-7">[7]</a></sup> The Polynesians also introduced the <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_rat" title="Polynesian rat">Polynesian rat</a>. This may have put some pressure on other birds but at the time of early European contact (18th Century) and colonisation (19th Century) the bird life was prolific. With them, the Europeans brought <a href="/wiki/Black_rat" title="Black rat">ship rats</a>, possums, cats and mustelids which decimated native bird life, some of which had adapted <a href="/wiki/Flightless_bird" title="Flightless bird">flightlessness</a> and ground nesting habits and others had no defensive behavior as a result of having no extant <a href="/wiki/Endemic" class="mw-redirect" title="Endemic">endemic</a> mammalian predators. The <a href="/wiki/Kakapo" title="Kakapo">kakapo</a>, the world's biggest parrot, which is flightless, now only exists in managed breeding sanctuaries and NZ's national emblem, the <a href="/wiki/Kiwi" title="Kiwi">kiwi</a>, is on the endangered bird list.<sup id="cite_ref-kolbert_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kolbert-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas">Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Americas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_North_American_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of North American animals extinct in the Holocene">List of North American animals extinct in the Holocene</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_South_American_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of South American animals extinct in the Holocene">List of South American animals extinct in the Holocene</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mammoth_House_(Replica).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Mammoth_House_%28Replica%29.JPG/220px-Mammoth_House_%28Replica%29.JPG" width="220" height="129" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Mammoth_House_%28Replica%29.JPG/330px-Mammoth_House_%28Replica%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Mammoth_House_%28Replica%29.JPG/440px-Mammoth_House_%28Replica%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3842" data-file-height="2252" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mammoth_House_(Replica).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Reconstructed <a href="/wiki/Woolly_mammoth" title="Woolly mammoth">woolly mammoth</a> bone hut, based on finds in <a href="/wiki/Mezhyrich" title="Mezhyrich">Mezhyrich</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There has been a debate as to the extent to which the disappearance of <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a> at the end of the last <a href="/wiki/Glacial_period" title="Glacial period">glacial period</a> can be attributed to human activities by hunting, or even by slaughter<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> of prey populations. Discoveries at Monte Verde in South America and at <a href="/wiki/Meadowcroft_Rock_Shelter" class="mw-redirect" title="Meadowcroft Rock Shelter">Meadowcroft Rock Shelter</a> in Pennsylvania have caused a controversy<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-59">[59]</a></sup> regarding the <a href="/wiki/Clovis_culture" title="Clovis culture">Clovis culture</a>. There likely would have been human settlements prior to the Clovis Culture, and the history of <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_the_Americas" title="Settlement of the Americas">humans in the Americas</a> may extend back many thousands of years before the Clovis culture.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-59">[59]</a></sup> The amount of correlation between human arrival and megafauna extinction is still being debated: for example, in <a href="/wiki/Wrangel_Island" title="Wrangel Island">Wrangel Island</a> in Siberia the extinction of dwarf <a href="/wiki/Woolly_mammoth" title="Woolly mammoth">woolly mammoths</a> (approximately 2000 BCE)<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> did not coincide with the arrival of humans, nor did megafaunal mass extinction on the South American continent, although it has been suggested climate changes induced by anthropogenic effects elsewhere in the world may have contributed.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Comparisons are sometimes made between recent extinctions (approximately since the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a>) and the Pleistocene extinction near the end of the last <a href="/wiki/Glacial_period" title="Glacial period">glacial period</a>. The latter is exemplified by the extinction of large herbivores such as the <a href="/wiki/Woolly_mammoth" title="Woolly mammoth">woolly mammoth</a> and the carnivores that preyed on them. Humans of this era actively hunted the <a href="/wiki/Mammoth" title="Mammoth">mammoth</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mastodon" title="Mastodon">mastodon</a><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> but it is not known if this hunting was the cause of the subsequent massive ecological changes, widespread extinctions and climate changes.<sup id="cite_ref-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-link.springer.com_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-link.springer.com-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ecosystems encountered by the first Americans had not been exposed to human interaction, and may have been far less resilient to human made changes than the ecosystems encountered by industrial era humans. Therefore, the actions of the Clovis people, despite seeming insignificant by today's standards could indeed have had a profound effect on the ecosystems and wild life which was entirely unused to human influence.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Afroeurasia">Afroeurasia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Afroeurasia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_African_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of African animals extinct in the Holocene">List of African animals extinct in the Holocene</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Asian_animals_extinct_in_the_Holocene" title="List of Asian animals extinct in the Holocene">List of Asian animals extinct in the Holocene</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_animals_of_Europe" title="List of extinct animals of Europe">List of extinct animals of Europe</a></div>
<p>Africa experienced the smallest decline in megafauna compared to the other continents. This is presumably due to the idea that Afroeurasian megafauna evolved alongside humans, and thus developed a healthy fear of them, unlike the comparatively tame animals of other continents.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Unlike other continents, the megafauna of Eurasia went extinct over a relatively long period of time, possibly due to climate fluctuations fragmenting and decreasing populations, leaving them vulnerable to over-exploitation, as with the <a href="/wiki/Steppe_bison" title="Steppe bison">steppe bison</a> (<i>Bison priscus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The warming of the arctic region caused the rapid decline of grasslands, which had a negative effect on the grazing megafauna of Eurasia. Most of what once was <a href="/wiki/Mammoth_steppe" title="Mammoth steppe">mammoth steppe</a> has been converted to <a href="/wiki/Mire" title="Mire">mire</a>, rendering the environment incapable of supporting them, notably the <a href="/wiki/Woolly_mammoth" title="Woolly mammoth">woolly mammoth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg/220px-Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg" width="220" height="392" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg/330px-Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg/440px-Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg 2x" data-file-width="558" data-file-height="994" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aridity_ice_age_vs_early_holocene_vs_modern.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Top:</i> <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">Arid</a> ice age climate
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<i>Middle:</i> <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic period">Atlantic Period</a>, warm and wet
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<i>Bottom:</i> Potential vegetation in climate now if not for human effects like agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-ORNL_paleoclimate_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ORNL_paleoclimate-65">[65]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One of the main theories to the extinction is <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>. The climate change theory has suggested that a change in climate near the end of the late Pleistocene stressed the megafauna to the point of extinction.<sup id="cite_ref-Zalasiewicz_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zalasiewicz-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Some scientists favor abrupt climate change as the catalyst for the extinction of the mega-fauna at the end of the Pleistocene, but there are many who believe increased hunting from early modern humans also played a part, with others even suggesting that the two interacted.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> However, the annual mean temperature of the current <a href="/wiki/Interglacial_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Interglacial period">interglacial period</a> for the last 10,000 years is no higher than that of previous interglacial periods, yet some of the same megafauna survived similar temperature increases.<sup id="cite_ref-Andersen1973_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andersen1973-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ashworth1980_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashworth1980-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Birks1973_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birks1973-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Birks_.26_Birks1980_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birks_.26_Birks1980-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bradely1985_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bradely1985-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davis1976_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davis1976-74">[74]</a></sup> In the Americas, a controversial explanation for the shift in climate is presented under the <a href="/wiki/Younger_Dryas_impact_hypothesis" title="Younger Dryas impact hypothesis">Younger Dryas impact hypothesis</a>, which states that the impact of comets cooled global temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-Firestone2006_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Firestone2006-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PNAS07A_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS07A-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bunch_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bunch-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Megafaunal_extinction">Megafaunal extinction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Megafaunal extinction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Megafauna play a significant role in the lateral transport of mineral nutrients in an ecosystem, tending to translocate them from areas of high to those of lower abundance. They do so by their movement between the time they consume the nutrient and the time they release it through elimination (or, to a much lesser extent, through decomposition after death).<sup id="cite_ref-Wolf2013_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolf2013-78">[78]</a></sup> In South America's <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Basin" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon Basin">Amazon Basin</a>, it is estimated that such lateral diffusion was reduced over 98% following the megafaunal extinctions that occurred roughly 12,500 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-Marshall_2013_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall_2013-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DoughtyWolf2013_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DoughtyWolf2013-80">[80]</a></sup> Given that <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> availability is thought to limit productivity in much of the region, the decrease in its transport from the western part of the basin and from floodplains (both of which derive their supply from the uplift of the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a>) to other areas is thought to have significantly impacted the region's ecology, and the effects may not yet have reached their limits.<sup id="cite_ref-DoughtyWolf2013_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DoughtyWolf2013-80">[80]</a></sup> The extinction of the <a href="/wiki/Mammoth" title="Mammoth">mammoths</a> allowed <a href="/wiki/Grasslands" class="mw-redirect" title="Grasslands">grasslands</a> they had maintained through grazing habits to become birch forests.<sup id="cite_ref-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010-5">[5]</a></sup> The new forest and the resulting forest fires may have induced <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doughty.2C_C._E._2010-5">[5]</a></sup> Such disappearances might be the result of the <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">proliferation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Modern_human" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern human">modern humans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-VanishingFauna_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VanishingFauna-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Large populations of megaherbivores have the potential to contribute greatly to the atmospheric concentration of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, which is an important <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a>. Modern <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminant</a> <a href="/wiki/Herbivores" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbivores">herbivores</a> produce methane as a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Foregut_fermentation" title="Foregut fermentation">foregut fermentation</a> in digestion, and release it through belching or flatulence. Today, around 20% of annual methane emissions come from livestock methane release. In the <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a>, it has been estimated that <a href="/wiki/Sauropod" class="mw-redirect" title="Sauropod">sauropods</a> could have emitted 520 million tons of methane to the atmosphere annually,<sup id="cite_ref-Wilkinson_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkinson-81">[81]</a></sup> contributing to the warmer climate of the time (up to 10&#160;°C warmer than at present).<sup id="cite_ref-Wilkinson_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkinson-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sauropod_methane_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sauropod_methane-82">[82]</a></sup> This large emission follows from the enormous estimated biomass of sauropods, and because methane production of individual herbivores is believed to be almost proportional to their mass.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilkinson_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkinson-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recent studies have indicated that the extinction of megafaunal herbivores may have caused a reduction in <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane" title="Atmospheric methane">atmospheric methane</a>. This hypothesis is relatively new.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-83">[83]</a></sup> One study examined the methane emissions from the <a href="/wiki/American_bison" title="American bison">bison</a> that occupied the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a> of North America before contact with European settlers. The study estimated that the removal of the bison caused a decrease of as much as 2.2 million tons per year.<sup id="cite_ref-Kelliher_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kelliher-84">[84]</a></sup> Another study examined the change in the methane concentration in the atmosphere at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene_epoch" class="mw-redirect" title="Pleistocene epoch">Pleistocene epoch</a> after the extinction of megafauna in the Americas. After early humans migrated to the Americas about 13,000 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>, their hunting and other associated ecological impacts led to the extinction of many megafaunal species there. Calculations suggest that this extinction decreased methane production by about 9.6 million tons per year. This suggests that the absence of megafaunal methane emissions may have contributed to the abrupt climatic cooling at the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Younger_Dryas" title="Younger Dryas">Younger Dryas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-83">[83]</a></sup> The decrease in atmospheric methane that occurred at that time, as recorded in <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">ice cores</a>, was 2-4 times more rapid than any other decrease in the last half million years, suggesting that an unusual mechanism was at work.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disease">Disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The hyperdisease hypothesis, proposed by Ross MacPhee in 1997, states that the megafaunal die-off was due to an indirect transmission of diseases by newly arriving <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">aboriginal humans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MacPhee.26Marx1997_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacPhee.26Marx1997-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MacPhee.26Marx1998_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacPhee.26Marx1998-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> According to MacPhee, aboriginals or animals travelling with them, such as <a href="/wiki/Domestic_dog" class="mw-redirect" title="Domestic dog">domestic dogs</a> or livestock, introduced one or more highly <a href="/wiki/Virulent" class="mw-redirect" title="Virulent">virulent</a> diseases into new environments whose native population had no immunity to, eventually leading to their extinction. <a href="/wiki/K-selection" class="mw-redirect" title="K-selection">K-selection</a> animals, such as the now-extinct megafauna, are especially vulnerable to diseases, as opposed to <a href="/wiki/R-selection" class="mw-redirect" title="R-selection">r-selection</a> animals who have a shorter <a href="/wiki/Gestation_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Gestation period">gestation period</a> and a higher population size. Humans are thought to be the sole cause as other earlier migrations of animals into North America from Eurasia did not cause extinctions.<sup id="cite_ref-MacPhee.26Marx1997_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacPhee.26Marx1997-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are many problems with this theory in the scientific community, as this disease would have to meet several criteria: it has to be able to sustain itself in an environment with no <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">hosts</a>; it has to have a high <a href="/wiki/Infection_rate" title="Infection rate">infection rate</a>; and be extremely lethal, with a <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality rate</a> of 50–75%. Disease has to be very virulent to kill off all the individuals in a <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> or <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>, and even such a virulent disease as <a href="/wiki/West_Nile_Virus" class="mw-redirect" title="West Nile Virus">West Nile Virus</a> is unlikely to have caused extinction.<sup id="cite_ref-Lyons_et_al2004_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lyons_et_al2004-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, diseases have been the cause for some extinctions. The introduction of <a href="/wiki/Avian_malaria" title="Avian malaria">avian malaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avipoxvirus" title="Avipoxvirus">avipoxvirus</a>, for example, have had a negative impact on the <a href="/wiki/Endemic_birds_of_Hawaii" title="Endemic birds of Hawaii">endemic birds of Hawaii</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LaPointe_2012_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LaPointe_2012-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Defaunation">Defaunation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Defaunation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bufo_periglenes2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Bufo_periglenes2.jpg/250px-Bufo_periglenes2.jpg" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Bufo_periglenes2.jpg/375px-Bufo_periglenes2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Bufo_periglenes2.jpg/500px-Bufo_periglenes2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3576" data-file-height="2385" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bufo_periglenes2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Golden_toad" title="Golden toad">golden toad</a> of Costa Rica, extinct since around 1989. Its disappearance has been attributed to a confluence of several factors, including <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">El Niño</a> warming, fungus, habitat loss and the introduction of invasive species.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Susa_group,_mountain_gorilla.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Susa_group%2C_mountain_gorilla.jpg/220px-Susa_group%2C_mountain_gorilla.jpg" width="220" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Susa_group%2C_mountain_gorilla.jpg/330px-Susa_group%2C_mountain_gorilla.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Susa_group%2C_mountain_gorilla.jpg/440px-Susa_group%2C_mountain_gorilla.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1552" data-file-height="1632" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Susa_group,_mountain_gorilla.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
There are roughly 880 <a href="/wiki/Mountain_gorillas" class="mw-redirect" title="Mountain gorillas">mountain gorillas</a> remaining in existence. 60% of <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primate</a> species face an anthropogenically driven extinction crisis and 75% have declining populations.<sup id="cite_ref-primates_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primates-91">[91]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_White_Rhinoceros_Angalifu.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Northern_White_Rhinoceros_Angalifu.jpg/220px-Northern_White_Rhinoceros_Angalifu.jpg" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Northern_White_Rhinoceros_Angalifu.jpg/330px-Northern_White_Rhinoceros_Angalifu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Northern_White_Rhinoceros_Angalifu.jpg/440px-Northern_White_Rhinoceros_Angalifu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2968" data-file-height="2152" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_White_Rhinoceros_Angalifu.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Angalifu_(rhinoceros)" class="mw-redirect" title="Angalifu (rhinoceros)">Angalifu</a>, a male <a href="/wiki/Northern_white_rhinoceros" title="Northern white rhinoceros">northern white rhinoceros</a> at the <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Zoo_Safari_Park" title="San Diego Zoo Safari Park">San Diego Zoo Safari Park</a> (died December 2014).<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Currently there are only three remaining alive on earth.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Defaunation" title="Defaunation">Defaunation</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Colony_collapse_disorder" title="Colony collapse disorder">Colony collapse disorder</a></div>
<p>The loss of species from ecological communities, defaunation, is primarily driven by human activity.<sup id="cite_ref-dirzo_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dirzo-3">[3]</a></sup> This has resulted in <a href="/wiki/Empty_forest" title="Empty forest">empty forests</a>, ecological communities depleted of large vertebrates.<sup id="cite_ref-primack2014_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primack2014-94">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> This is not to be confused with extinction, as it includes both the disappearance of species and declines in abundance.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Defaunation effects were first implied at the Symposium of Plant-Animal Interactions at the University of Campinas, Brazil in 1988 in the context of <a href="/wiki/Neotropic_ecozone" class="mw-redirect" title="Neotropic ecozone">neotropical forests</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-eco_and_evo_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eco_and_evo-97">[97]</a></sup> Since then, the term has gained broader usage in conservation biology as a global phenomenon.<sup id="cite_ref-def_ant_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-def_ant-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-eco_and_evo_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eco_and_evo-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Big_cat" title="Big cat">Big cat</a> populations have been decimated over the last half-century and could face extinction in the following decades. According to <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_the_Conservation_of_Nature" class="mw-redirect" title="International Union for the Conservation of Nature">IUCN</a> estimates: <a href="/wiki/Lions" class="mw-redirect" title="Lions">lions</a> are down to 25,000, from 450,000; <a href="/wiki/Leopards" class="mw-redirect" title="Leopards">leopards</a> are down to 50,000, from 750,000; <a href="/wiki/Cheetahs" class="mw-redirect" title="Cheetahs">cheetahs</a> are down to 12,000, from 45,000; <a href="/wiki/Tigers" class="mw-redirect" title="Tigers">tigers</a> are down to 3,000 in the wild, from 50,000.<sup id="cite_ref-bigcats_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bigcats-99">[99]</a></sup> A December 2016 study by the Zoological Society of London, <a href="/wiki/Panthera_Corporation" title="Panthera Corporation">Panthera Corporation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_Conservation_Society" title="Wildlife Conservation Society">Wildlife Conservation Society</a> showed that cheetahs are far closer to extinction than previously thought, with only 7,100 remaining in the wild, and crammed within only 9% of their historic range.<sup id="cite_ref-Visser_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Visser-100">[100]</a></sup> Human pressures are to blame for the cheetah population crash, including prey loss due to overhunting by people, retaliatory killing from farmers, habitat loss and the illegal wildlife trade.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">We are seeing the effects of 7 billion people on the planet. At present rates, we will lose the big cats in 10 to 15 years.</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" class="cquotecite" style="border: none; padding-right: 4%; font-size: smaller; text-align: right;"><cite>— Naturalist Dereck Joubert, co-founder of the <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic</a> Big Cats Initiative<sup id="cite_ref-bigcats_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bigcats-99">[99]</a></sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
