<p>The term <a href="/wiki/Pollinator_decline" title="Pollinator decline">pollinator decline</a> refers to the reduction in abundance of insect and other animal pollinators in many ecosystems worldwide beginning at the end of the twentieth century, and continuing into the present day.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Pollinators, which are necessary for 75% of food crops, are declining globally in both abundance and diversity.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">We have driven the rate of biological extinction, the permanent loss of species, up several hundred times beyond its historical levels, and are threatened with the loss of a majority of all species by the end of the 21st century.</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" class="cquotecite" style="border: none; padding-right: 4%; font-size: smaller; text-align: right;"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Peter_H._Raven" title="Peter H. Raven">Peter Raven</a>, former president of the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Science" title="American Association for the Advancement of Science">American Association for the Advancement of Science</a> (AAAS), in the foreword to their publication <i>AAAS Atlas of Population and Environment</i><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Various species are predicted to <a href="/wiki/List_of_critically_endangered_species" class="mw-redirect" title="List of critically endangered species">become extinct in the near future</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> among them the <a href="/wiki/Rhinoceros" title="Rhinoceros">rhinoceros</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Primates" class="mw-redirect" title="Primates">primates</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-primates_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primates-91">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pangolins" class="mw-redirect" title="Pangolins">pangolins</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Giraffes" class="mw-redirect" title="Giraffes">giraffes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Hunting alone threatens hundreds of mammalian species around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Scientists claim that the growing demand for <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meat</a> is contributing to biodiversity loss as this is a significant driver of <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> and habitat destruction; species-rich habitats, such as significant portions of the Amazon region, are being converted to agriculture for meat production.<sup id="cite_ref-Zalasiewicz2015_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zalasiewicz2015-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Fund" class="mw-redirect" title="World Wildlife Fund">World Wildlife Fund</a>'s 2016 <a href="/wiki/Living_Planet_Index" title="Living Planet Index">Living Planet Index</a>, global wildlife populations have declined 58% since 1970, primarily due to habitat destruction, over-hunting and pollution. They project that if current trends continue, 67% of wildlife could disappear by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> 189 countries, which are signatory to the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_Biological_Diversity" title="Convention on Biological Diversity">Convention on Biological Diversity</a> (Rio Accord),<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> have committed to preparing a <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_Action_Plan" class="mw-redirect" title="Biodiversity Action Plan">Biodiversity Action Plan</a>, a first step at identifying specific <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered species</a> and habitats, country by country.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">For the first time since the demise of the dinosaurs 65 million years ago, we face a global mass extinction of wildlife. We ignore the decline of other species at our peril – for they are the barometer that reveals our impact on the world that sustains us.</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" class="cquotecite" style="border: none; padding-right: 4%; font-size: smaller; text-align: right;"><cite>— Mike Barrett, director of science and policy at WWF's UK branch<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_extinction">Recent extinction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Recent extinction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List_extinct_in_the_wild_species" title="IUCN Red List extinct in the wild species">IUCN Red List extinct in the wild species</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_endangered_species" class="mw-redirect" title="List of endangered species">List of endangered species</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_critically_endangered_species" class="mw-redirect" title="List of critically endangered species">List of critically endangered species</a></div>
<p>Recent extinctions are more directly attributable to human influences, whereas prehistoric extinctions can be attributed to other factors, such as global climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-ceballos_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ceballos-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dirzo_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dirzo-3">[3]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN) characterises 'recent' extinction as those that have occurred past the cut-off point of 1500,<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> and at least 875 species have gone extinct since that time and 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Some species, such as the <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A8re_David%27s_deer" title="Père David's deer">Père David's deer</a><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_crow" title="Hawaiian crow">Hawaiian crow</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-121">[121]</a></sup> are extinct in the wild, and survive solely in captive populations. Other species, such as the <a href="/wiki/Florida_panther" title="Florida panther">Florida panther</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Ecological_extinction" title="Ecological extinction">ecologically extinct</a>, surviving in such low numbers that that they essentially have no impact on the ecosystem.<sup id="cite_ref-mckinney2013_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mckinney2013-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:318</sup> Other populations are only <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">locally extinct</a> (extirpated), still existence elsewhere, but reduced in distribution,<sup id="cite_ref-mckinney2013_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mckinney2013-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:75–77</sup> as with the extinction of <a href="/wiki/Gray_whale" title="Gray whale">gray whales</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> and of the <a href="/wiki/Leatherback_sea_turtle" title="Leatherback sea turtle">leatherback sea turtle</a> in Malaysia.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat_destruction">Habitat destruction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Habitat destruction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Extinction_risk_from_global_warming" title="Extinction risk from global warming">Extinction risk from global warming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">Ocean acidification</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marine_pollution" title="Marine pollution">Marine pollution</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Social_and_environmental_impact_of_palm_oil" title="Social and environmental impact of palm oil">Social and environmental impact of palm oil</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bramble-cay-melomys.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Bramble-cay-melomys.jpg/220px-Bramble-cay-melomys.jpg" width="220" height="114" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Bramble-cay-melomys.jpg/330px-Bramble-cay-melomys.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Bramble-cay-melomys.jpg/440px-Bramble-cay-melomys.jpg 2x" data-file-width="770" data-file-height="400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bramble-cay-melomys.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bramble_Cay_melomys" title="Bramble Cay melomys">Bramble Cay melomys</a> were declared extinct in June 2016. This is likely the first mammalian extinction due to <a href="/wiki/Anthropogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthropogenic">anthropogenic</a> climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Global warming is widely accepted as being a contributor to extinction worldwide, in a similar way that <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">previous extinction events</a> have generally included a rapid change in global climate and meteorology. It is also expected to disrupt sex ratios in many reptiles which have <a href="/wiki/Temperature-dependent_sex_determination" title="Temperature-dependent sex determination">temperature-dependent sex determination</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Malayasia_iko_2002169.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Malayasia_iko_2002169.jpg/250px-Malayasia_iko_2002169.jpg" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Malayasia_iko_2002169.jpg/375px-Malayasia_iko_2002169.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Malayasia_iko_2002169.jpg/500px-Malayasia_iko_2002169.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Malayasia_iko_2002169.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Satellite image of rainforest converted to <a href="/wiki/Oil_palm" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil palm">oil palm</a> plantations.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The removal of land to clear way for <a href="/wiki/Social_and_environmental_impact_of_palm_oil" title="Social and environmental impact of palm oil">palm oil plantations</a> releases carbon emissions held in the <a href="/wiki/Peatland" class="mw-redirect" title="Peatland">peatlands</a> of Indonesia.<sup id="cite_ref-Foster_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foster-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rosenthal_NYT_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenthal_NYT-128">[128]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">Palm oil</a> mainly serves as a cheap <a href="/wiki/Cooking_oil" title="Cooking oil">cooking oil</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> and also as a (<a href="/wiki/Food_vs._fuel" title="Food vs. fuel">controversial</a>) <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a>. However, damage to peatland contributes to 4% of global greenhouse gas emissions, and 8% of those caused by burning <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Palm oil cultivation has also been criticized for other impacts to the environment,<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Greenpeace_Cooking_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenpeace_Cooking-132">[132]</a></sup> including deforestation,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> which has threatened critically endangered species such as the <a href="/wiki/Orangutan" title="Orangutan">orangutan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cspi_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cspi-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mail_Online_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mail_Online-135">[135]</a></sup> The IUCN stated in 2016 that the species could go extinct within a decade if measures are not taken to preserve the rainforests in which they live.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rising levels of carbon dioxide are resulting in influx of this gas into the ocean, increasing its acidity. Marine organisms which possess Calcium Carbonate shells or exoskeletons experience physiological pressure as the carbonate reacts with acid. This is already resulting in <a href="/wiki/Coral_bleaching" title="Coral bleaching">coral bleaching</a> on various <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a> worldwide, which provide valuable habitat for very high biodiversity. Marine gastropods, bivalves and other invertebrates are also affected, as are any organisms that feed on them.</p>
<p>Some researchers suggest that by 2050 there could be more plastic than fish in the oceans by weight.<sup id="cite_ref-Sutter_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sutter-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overexploitation">Overexploitation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Overexploitation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Species_affected_by_poaching" title="Species affected by poaching">Species affected by poaching</a> and <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">Overfishing</a></div>
<p>Overhunting can reduce the local population of <a href="/wiki/Game_(hunting)" title="Game (hunting)">game animals</a> by more than half, as well as reducing population density, and may lead to extinction for some species.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Populations located nearer to villages are significantly more at risk of depletion.<sup id="cite_ref-peres2006_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peres2006-138">[138]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>The surge in the mass killings by <a href="/wiki/Poachers" class="mw-redirect" title="Poachers">poachers</a> involved in the illegal ivory trade along with habitat loss is threatening <a href="/wiki/African_elephant" title="African elephant">African elephant</a> populations.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sevenyears_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sevenyears-141">[141]</a></sup> In 1979, their populations stood at 1.7 million; at present there are fewer than 400,000 remaining.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Prior to European colonization, scientists believe Africa was home to roughly 20 million elephants.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Elephant_Census" title="Great Elephant Census">Great Elephant Census</a>, 30% of African elephants (or 144,000 individuals) disappeared over a seven-year period, 2007 to 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-sevenyears_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sevenyears-141">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> African elephants could become extinct within 20 years if poaching rates continue.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:283px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg/281px-Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg" width="281" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg/422px-Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg/562px-Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg 2x" data-file-width="942" data-file-height="738" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Surexploitation_morue_surp%C3%AAcheEn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The collapse of <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_cod" title="Atlantic cod">Atlantic cod</a> off the coast of Newfoundland in 1992 as a result of <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a>. The population never recovered, completely altering the ecosystem and rendering the species <a href="/wiki/Functional_extinction" title="Functional extinction">locally extinct</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Fishing has had a devastating effect on marine organism populations for several centuries even before the explosion of destructive and highly effective fishing practices like <a href="/wiki/Trawling" title="Trawling">trawling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Humans are unique among predators in that they regularly predate on other adult <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predators</a>, particularly in marine environments;<sup id="cite_ref-:4_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-35">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bluefin_tuna" title="Bluefin tuna">bluefin tuna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue_whales" class="mw-redirect" title="Blue whales">blue whales</a>, and various <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a> in particular are particularly vulnerable to predation pressure from human fishing. A 2016 study published in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i> concludes that humans tend to hunt larger species, and this could disrupt ocean ecosystems for millions of years.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">If this pattern goes unchecked, the future oceans would lack many of the largest species in today’s oceans. Many large species play critical roles in ecosystems and so their extinctions could lead to ecological cascades that would influence the structure and function of future ecosystems beyond the simple fact of losing those species.</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" class="cquotecite" style="border: none; padding-right: 4%; font-size: smaller; text-align: right;"><cite>— Jonathan Payne, associate professor and chair of geological sciences at Stanford University<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disease_2">Disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holocene_extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Decline_in_amphibian_populations" title="Decline in amphibian populations">Decline in amphibian populations</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_nose_syndrome" class="mw-redirect" title="White nose syndrome">White nose syndrome</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ecnomiohyla_rabborum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Ecnomiohyla_rabborum.jpg/220px-Ecnomiohyla_rabborum.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Ecnomiohyla_rabborum.jpg/330px-Ecnomiohyla_rabborum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Ecnomiohyla_rabborum.jpg/440px-Ecnomiohyla_rabborum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ecnomiohyla_rabborum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Toughie_(frog)" title="Toughie (frog)">Toughie</a>, the last <a href="/wiki/Rabbs%27_fringe-limbed_treefrog" class="mw-redirect" title="Rabbs' fringe-limbed treefrog">Rabbs' fringe-limbed treefrog</a>, died in September 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> The species was killed off from the <a href="/wiki/Chytrid_fungus" class="mw-redirect" title="Chytrid fungus">chytrid fungus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Batrachochytrium_dendrobatidis" title="Batrachochytrium dendrobatidis">Batrachochytrium dendrobatidis</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Decline_in_amphibian_populations" title="Decline in amphibian populations">decline of amphibian populations</a> has also been identified as an indicator of environmental degradation. As well as habitat loss, introduced predators and pollution, <a href="/wiki/Chytridiomycosis" title="Chytridiomycosis">Chytridiomycosis</a>, a fungal infection thought to have been accidentally spread by human travel,<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup> has caused severe population drops of several species of frogs, including (among many others) the extinction of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_toad" title="Golden toad">golden toad</a> in Costa Rica and the <a href="/wiki/Gastric-brooding_frog" title="Gastric-brooding frog">Gastric-brooding frog</a> in Australia. Many other amphibian species now face extinction, including the reduction of <a href="/wiki/Rabb%27s_Fringe-limbed_Treefrog" class="mw-redirect" title="Rabb's Fringe-limbed Treefrog">Rabb's fringe-limbed treefrog</a> to an <a href="/wiki/Endling" title="Endling">endling</a>, and the extinction of the <a href="/wiki/Panamanian_golden_frog" title="Panamanian golden frog">Panamanian golden frog</a> in the wild. Chytrid fungus has spread across Australia, New Zealand, Central America and Africa, including countries with high amphibian diversity such as <a href="/wiki/Cloud_forest" title="Cloud forest">cloud forests</a> in <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Batrachochytrium_salamandrivorans" title="Batrachochytrium salamandrivorans">Batrachochytrium salamandrivorans</a></i> is a similar infection currently threatening <a href="/wiki/Salamander" title="Salamander">salamanders</a>. Amphibians are now the most endangered vertebrate group, having existed for more than 300 million years through three other <a href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" class="mw-redirect" title="Mass extinction">mass extinctions</a>.</p>
<p>Millions of bats in the US have been dying off since 2012 due to a fungal infection spread from European bats, which appear to be immune. Population drops have been as great as 90% within five years, and extinction of at least one bat species is predicted. There is currently no form of treatment, and such declines have been described as "unprecedented" in bat evolutionary history by Alan Hicks of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Department_of_Environmental_Conservation" title="New York State Department of Environmental Conservation">New York State Department of Environmental Conservation</a>.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

"Effect of per-capita land use changes on Holocene forest clearance and CO<sub>2</sub> emissions.". <i>Quaternary Science Reviews</i>. <b>28</b>: 3011–3015. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.quascirev.2009.05.022">10.1016/j.quascirev.2009.05.022</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHolocene+extinction&amp;rft.atitle=Effect+of+per-capita+land+use+changes+on+Holocene+forest+clearance+and+CO%3Csub%3E2%3C%2Fsub%3E+emissions.&amp;rft.aufirst=W.F.&amp;rft.aulast=Ruddiman&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.quascirev.2009.05.022&amp;rft.jtitle=Quaternary+Science+Reviews&amp;rft.pages=3011-3015&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=28" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> <span style="display:none;font-size:100%" class="error citation-comment"><code style="color:inherit; border:inherit; padding:inherit;">|access-date=</code> requires <code style="color:inherit; border:inherit; padding:inherit;">|url=</code> (<a href="/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#accessdate_missing_url" title="Help:CS1 errors">help</a>)</span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news"><a href="/wiki/Yuval_Noah_Harari" title="Yuval Noah Harari">Harari, Yuval N.</a> (September 25, 2015). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.theguardian.com/books/2015/sep/25/industrial-farming-one-worst-crimes-history-ethical-question">"Industrial farming is one of the worst crimes in history"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">October 23,</span> 2016</span>. <q>Even tens of thousands of years ago, our stone age ancestors were already responsible for a series of ecological disasters. When the first humans reached Australia about 45,000 years ago, they quickly drove to extinction 90% of its large animals. This was the first significant impact that <i>Homo sapiens</i> had on the planet’s <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a>. It was not the last. About 15,000 years ago, humans colonised America, wiping out in the process about 75% of its large mammals. Numerous other species disappeared from Africa, from Eurasia and from the myriad islands around their coasts.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHolocene+extinction&amp;rft.atitle=Industrial+farming+is+one+of+the+worst+crimes+in+history&amp;rft.aufirst=Yuval+N.&amp;rft.aulast=Harari&amp;rft.date=2015-09-25&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.theguardian.com%2Fbooks%2F2015%2Fsep%2F25%2Findustrial-farming-one-worst-crimes-history-ethical-question&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Guardian&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
